    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4037925456734370964"><b>
  <i>As the Clone Wars rage, victory or defeat lies in the hands of elite squads that take on the toughest assignments in the galaxy–stone-cold soldiers who go where no one else would, to do what no one else could. . . .</i>
</b><br/><br/>On a mission to sabotage a chemical weapon research facility on a Separatist-held planet, four clone troopers operate under the very noses of their enemies.</span>
  <span id="freeText4037925456734370964" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>As the Clone Wars rage, victory or defeat lies in the hands of elite squads that take on the toughest assignments in the galaxy–stone-cold soldiers who go where no one else would, to do what no one else could. . . .</i>
</b><br /><br />On a mission to sabotage a chemical weapon research facility on a Separatist-held planet, four clone troopers operate under the very noses of their enemies. The commandos are outnumbered and outgunned, deep behind enemy lines with no backup–and working with strangers instead of trusted teammates. Matters don’t improve when Darman, the squad’s demolitions expert, gets separated from the others during planetfall. Even Darman’s apparent good luck in meeting an inexperienced Padawan vanishes once Etain admits to her woeful inexperience.<br /><br />For the separated clone commandos and stranded Jedi, a long, dangerous journey lies ahead, through hostile territory brimming with Trandoshan slavers, Separatists, and suspicious natives. A single misstep could mean discovery . . . and death. It’s a virtual suicide mission for anyone–anyone except Republic Commandos.</span>

<div id="review_19113729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19113729" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1041498-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523985929p2/1041498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19113729?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/1041498-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1041498-ryan?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19113729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12214033718115611762">When is a Star Wars novel not a Star Wars novel? <br/><br/>That seems to be one of the questions raised by Republic Commando: Hard Contact. On the surface, it very much seems to be one - you have a group of Commando clone troopers trying to rescue a young Jedi from a Mandalorian warrior, and the Clone Wars are very actively happening in the background of the novel's action. Despite these surface similarities, however, the novel bears very little in common with the rest of the Star Wars universe, and what </span>
  <span id="freeText12214033718115611762" style="display:none">When is a Star Wars novel not a Star Wars novel? <br /><br />That seems to be one of the questions raised by Republic Commando: Hard Contact. On the surface, it very much seems to be one - you have a group of Commando clone troopers trying to rescue a young Jedi from a Mandalorian warrior, and the Clone Wars are very actively happening in the background of the novel's action. Despite these surface similarities, however, the novel bears very little in common with the rest of the Star Wars universe, and what is the "house style" of the EU (Expanded Universe) novels in general. If a few names and titles had have been changed, this could have very easily been an 'original fiction' title, rather than a tie-in novel. For all of the stories that have been written in the Star Wars universe, most of them have focused very heavily on the 'Star' portion of the title, with little if any focus done on the 'Wars' part. Hard Contact changes that by delivering a story of military sci-fi, focusing on an elite group of Commando clones.<br /><br />Throughout the course of the novel, Traviss does a very good job of developing all of her clone soldiers as distinct individuals. In other parts of the Clone Wars saga, the clones exist more or less as cyphers; in addition to sharing a genetic code, it appeared that they all shared a character (or lack thereof). One of the themes that Traviss explores is the idea of finding both a culture and an individual identity, and the difficulties that would be associated with that, when there are millions of others who share both your physical form and most of your personal past. [return][return]I generally try not to be a snob when it comes to liscenced material, but in the past year I've read a few that were really a chore to get through. Hard Contact is the exact opposite of that; with or without the Star Wars name attached to it, it was a really enjoyable piece of fiction writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12214033718115611762" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3122607893" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3122607893" />
    <a title="Para (wanderer)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17550719-para-wanderer"><img alt="Para (wanderer)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490137600p2/17550719.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3122607893?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Para (wanderer)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Para (wanderer)" href="/user/show/17550719-para-wanderer">Para (wanderer)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17550719-para-wanderer?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17550719-para-wanderer?shelf=military">military</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17550719-para-wanderer?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17550719-para-wanderer?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17550719-para-wanderer?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17550719-para-wanderer?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17550719-para-wanderer?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17550719-para-wanderer?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3122607893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7018353394585701059">I admit, before I started my Star Wars binge I had more than a few prejudices about tie-ins (just ask all my friends who had to listen to me whining about the tie-in r/Fantasy Bingo square for months on end). I heard bad things about quality. And I just never saw the point. Sure I have played video games that had tie-ins before, but I've never really cared about video game lore. But Star Wars has left me with a few specific itches I desperately want scratched, no matter how, plus a desperate nee</span>
  <span id="freeText7018353394585701059" style="display:none">I admit, before I started my Star Wars binge I had more than a few prejudices about tie-ins (just ask all my friends who had to listen to me whining about the tie-in r/Fantasy Bingo square for months on end). I heard bad things about quality. And I just never saw the point. Sure I have played video games that had tie-ins before, but I've never really cared about video game lore. But Star Wars has left me with a few specific itches I desperately want scratched, no matter how, plus a desperate need for <em>more</em>. Tie-ins, fanfic, tv series, canon or non-canon, give me all of it.<br /><br />Luckily, <em>Hard Contact</em> is a damn good book that went above and beyond my expectations. I came in for an easy read with clone troopers and Mandalorian culture (which it was!) and stayed for the thorough examination of how fucked up the concept of a clone army really is and the wonderful (at times even adorable!) interactions between the characters. In other words, it was an absolute delight and I can easily see myself devouring the rest of the series.<br /><br />The plot is simple. Four clone commandos whose previous squad members were killed are assembled into a new squad and sent on a mission to destroy a facility where a virus targeting clones is being developed. On their way, they get separated and Darman has a run-in with a stranded, extremely inexperienced Padawan who is forced to become their commander because she is a Jedi and they were made to obey Jedi.<br /><br />If you ever wondered why nobody in the prequels ever pointed at the clone troopers and started wondering how the Republic can still call themselves the good guys and do this, if you didn't like how they were dehumanised blaster fodder...well, then this is definitely the book for you. As a call-out it's downright brutal.<br /><br />Traviss takes the concept of what happens when you use people as disposable to its logical conclusion. She doesn't exactly have a delicate touch about it, she shows us first and then tells as well just to hammer the point home, but for once, I didn't mind a lack of subtlety. I had to see someone notice the same issues I noticed, the point desperately had to be made, plus I have a huge weakness for stories humanising and giving voice to the faceless minor characters, and I got all of that in spades. Darman gets the most character development out of the bunch, with Atin close behind, but they're all very clearly not identical. And they have a great dynamic. It reminded me a little of the Bridgeburners or the Bonehunters from <em>The Malazan Book of the Fallen</em>, another series that focuses on soldiers.<br /><br />All of this goes double if you also want stories that don't paint Jedi as flawless paragons. Etain, the Padawan forced into a commander role, is a wonderful character and I love her to bits. She's woefully inexperienced, reeling from the death of her master, far from fully trained, far from the best Force or lightsaber user, and insecure about it all on top of it, but dammit, she tries her absolute best. She <em>cares</em>, perhaps too much for a Jedi. And she learns fast. Her interactions with Darman are oddly heartwarming and heartbreaking at the same time, and definitely the highlight of the book for me.<br /><br />And then there are little snippets of Mandalorian culture interspersed throughout! <a href="https://otherworldsreviews.wordpress.com/2019/12/30/star-wars-without-nostalgia-the-mandalorian/" rel="nofollow noopener">Given my entry point into Star Wars</a>, I have an obvious fondness for Mandos. Here it mostly shows up in two forms - the POV of the main antagonist Hokan, who is a Mandalorian, and the clone commandos themselves, who were trained by Mandalorians. Hokan himself is interesting, very much not evil for the sake of evil (and antagonists who are not evil for the sake of evil are awesome). He's as disturbed by the concept of clones - of one of his own people, no less - as the reader, though obviously his solution to the problem is <em>way</em> too extreme. And he definitely doesn't see them as people. But the motivation? The motivation makes perfect sense. Unfortunately, I thought he was a little wasted in the second half when his POVs stopped being as interspersed with his views and opinions and generally <em>him</em> as a character and became almost completely about tactics and exposition. But aside from that, and a rather unfortunate nickname for the Trandoshans used in one of the last chapters (see: content warning section below), no real complaints.<br /><br />It's just such a wonderfully character-focused book. Sure, there's plenty of action and explosions. The writing style is very simple and straightforward and it's not even remotely slow-paced. But in my reviews of the movies and discussion around them, I have gone on and on about how action is meaningless without slow moments that establish why you should care - and here the balance was perfect (though unlike with movies, I'm all about slow moments when it comes to books). I may be more than a little blind to its flaws, but what the hell. It entertained me, it provided the worldbuilding I wanted, it made me think, it even broke my heart a little. What else could I ever possibly want?<br /><br /><strong>Enjoyment:</strong> 5/5<br /><strong>Execution:</strong> 4/5<br /><br /><strong>Recommended to:</strong> fellow Star Wars fans who don't care about canon, anyone who thinks the Mandalorians are cool, those who wondered if nobody in the prequels saw how fucked up the concept of clone troopers is, fans of military SFF, the found family trope, or character-focused books, anyone looking for a fast and easy read, those who wonder how can a book manage to combine "fucked up" and "adorable"<br /><strong>Not recommended to:</strong> those who dislike the idea of Jedi being flawed, those who dislike made-up swearwords (though to be fair, at least Mando'a is a proper conlang), plus see content warnings below<br /><br /><strong>
  <u>Content warnings:</u>
</strong> Attempted rape at the beginning. Also, it contains an extremely unfortunate nickname for the Trandoshans that is very similar to a slur. It only appears in one scene near the end and I genuinely believe it was not intended to offend or cause harm (besides, the book was published in 2004) - which is why I did not reduce the rating - but it threw me out of the story and deserves a strong warning for those who require it regardless.<br /><br /><i>More reviews on my blog, <a href="https://otherworldsreviews.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">To Other Worlds</a>.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7018353394585701059" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115511449?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=sf-star-trek-star-wars-and-tv">sf-star-trek-star-wars-and-tv</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115511449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8227302130002490543">4.0 to 4.5 stars. This is an excellent example of a really good writer (which Karen Traviss certainly is) using the Star Wars universe as "background" but still taking the the time to tell a good story. If you were to remove the Star Wars elements from this book, it would still be a compelling read as the characters are fully fleshed out, the action scenes are very well written and the plot is excellent. It is books like this that show you what a rich, wonderful background the Star Wars universe</span>
  <span id="freeText8227302130002490543" style="display:none">4.0 to 4.5 stars. This is an excellent example of a really good writer (which Karen Traviss certainly is) using the Star Wars universe as "background" but still taking the the time to tell a good story. If you were to remove the Star Wars elements from this book, it would still be a compelling read as the characters are fully fleshed out, the action scenes are very well written and the plot is excellent. It is books like this that show you what a rich, wonderful background the Star Wars universe can provide. It also makes you angry that so many writers just "mail it in" knowing that the Star Wars brand will sell itself. Highly recommended!!</span>

<div id="review_111122607" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111122607" />
    <a title="Crystal Starr Light" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3990525-crystal-starr-light"><img alt="Crystal Starr Light" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573066911p2/3990525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/111122607?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Crystal Starr Light" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Crystal Starr Light" href="/user/show/3990525-crystal-starr-light">Crystal Starr Light</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3990525-crystal-starr-light?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3990525-crystal-starr-light?shelf=simply-the-best">simply-the-best</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111122607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13627588742866020414">"Not all soldiers wear uniforms, ma'am"<br/>Thanks to a Star Wars book club, I got to reread this amazing Clone Wars era novel!<br/>Four clone commandos have been assigned an important task: land on Qiilura, apprehend scientist, Uthan, and destroy her work on biological agents targeted at clones. But a Mandalorian mercenary, the desperate natives, and a green Padawan might keep them from their objective.<br/><br/>What I Liked:<br/>There is so much to love about this book, it's hard to decide where exactly to begin.<br/>I gue</span>
  <span id="freeText13627588742866020414" style="display:none">"Not all soldiers wear uniforms, ma'am"<br />Thanks to a Star Wars book club, I got to reread this amazing Clone Wars era novel!<br />Four clone commandos have been assigned an important task: land on Qiilura, apprehend scientist, Uthan, and destroy her work on biological agents targeted at clones. But a Mandalorian mercenary, the desperate natives, and a green Padawan might keep them from their objective.<br /><br />What I Liked:<br />There is so much to love about this book, it's hard to decide where exactly to begin.<br />I guess I should start at the beginning: the characters. So many books (even--or maybe especially?--Star Wars ones) tend to generalize. The sleuth is usually down and out but incredibly smart. The scientists are either sexy, sassy, ladies or complete dorky nerds. Jedi are omnipotent, all seeing. The bad guys are plum out of a children's book (replete with giggling over their latest plot device, which is lame, lame, lame). Personally, I am sick of this. I want to see REAL people. People who are like me. So-so at their jobs, average in looks, maybe not quite happy with themselves (and not because they don't have any friends, being supermodel material after all--cue eye roll), trying so hard to figure out why the heck they are there.<br />And then Karen Traviss starts writing Star Wars. First off, THANK YOU DEL REY!!! Karen Traviss is a superb, intelligent writer and the perfect fit for the Republic Commando novels. She can do what so many others fail at (particularly in this book): she writes about real people.<br />Hokan is a mercenary. He is not a giggling bad guy, but an intelligent, well-planned Mandalorian who doesn't kill because it's fun but for a purpose. He even shows a fondness and appreciation for a subordinate (something sorely lacking in other novels).<br />Etain Tur-Mukan is probably one of the best written Jedi in the universe. Seriously, here is a woman with a conflict. She has no idea what to do, doesn't even think she can do it if she could, and isn't a super-Jedi either. She doubts, she questions, she is unsure. In fact, she totally blows up at Darman when he calls her commander and is later chewed out by Jinart. This is rarely done in the Star Wars universe and is a breath of fresh air in a sewer plant.<br />Then, the clones. Karen Traviss gets the award for making each clone identical yet separate. Niner is a no-bones leader. Fi is more sensitive, more worried about the feelings of each member. Atin buries his thoughts behind mechanical equipment. Darman is amazing, tender yet tough. Through him, we see the unique balance of hardened warrior and childlike innocence, something one would never have expected from clones bred for the sole purpose of killing another.<br />The other characters (Jinart, Uthan, etc.) are similarly well done, but in a much smaller scale, since they are more of background characters.<br />Moving on from characterization, the number of characters is perfect. We have, as primary characters, 4 clones, one Jedi, and one Mandalorian (Jinart, the Weequay, the other Jedi, and Uthan being secondary). There is just enough time to develop each character, not too much to interrupt the action and plot. One complaint I had about Death Star was its huge cast. It was challenging to get close to any of the characters when there were 20 or so of them. Karen Traviss keeps the cast small so the audience can grow close to them. It works perfectly.<br />I feel the balance in Hard Contact--characters, Mando'a history, philosophical musings on the usage of clones and the actual plot of the story--is perfect. KT spends enough time to make you feel for the characters, get acquainted to the Mandalorian lifestyle (which had never before been revealed), ponder the humanity of the clones (though this got to be a little much at times--see below), and do what commandos are supposed to do: Fight! The action in the story is prominent, cementing its Star Wars-iness, but while it is a big part of the book, KT doesn't cut back on character.<br />Another (small) favorite comes when Hokan mentions to Uthan (another ho-hum lady, not some sexy vixan--Thank you again, KT!!) about Hard Contact! I love it when the author slips in the title of her book somewhere in the actual book. I know it can't always be done, but this was totally awesome!!<br /><br />What I Didn't Like<br />It was very challenging to visualize the Gurlanins. They are vaguely described as having a snout, being black, and reminding me of either wolves or panthers. Other than that, their shapeshifting, their appearance is cloaked in mystery, making it hard to imagine the actual creature.<br />Another problem I had was towards the end. At this point, I felt the message of clones being real people was too heavy-handed. Etain mentions it at least twice in the last ten pages besides the conversations previous. I understand the concept and appreciate KT making the effort to talk about something this controversial, but I think she could have backed off a bit.<br />Finally, I do not appreciate Etain's being practically commended for defying a superior officer (when she is demanding the Laati stay on Qiilura until Fi and Niner return from their mission). Anywhere else, she would have been courtmarshalled for defying General Zey's orders. I understand this is a fiction novel; however, I feel that the scene could have been modified so as not to seemingly promote this fantastical scenario.<br /><br />Dialogue/Sexual Situations/Violence:<br />Star Wars dialogue in the form of "fierfek".<br />A Weequay attempts to rape Etain. Other similar acts are referred to circumspectly.<br />This is a story about commandos and battle. There are tons of battles, lots of gun fights, deaths, explosions, etc. While most aren't gory in nature, they do exist.<br /><br />Conclusions:<br />Amazing! KT has done for the Star Wars Clone Wars era--and for the Star Wars universe in general--what many of the other novels (The Cestus Decpetion and Jedi Trial) could only dream of doing. In Hard Contact, the audience is transported into the middle of a commando mission. There is no doubt about the authenticity, the realness of the surroundings, weapons, actions.<br />KT not only has created one of (if not the) best action novels, but she has also done the unbelievable: she has created a Mando'a culture, catapulted the clones into humans, and has portrayed Jedi in a real life sense.<br />With these amazing accomplishments under her belt, it would be impossible for me to give this novel anything less than a 5 stars, even with the minor road bumps. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13627588742866020414" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_33780928" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33780928" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1563047-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33780928?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1563047-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Any Star Wars or Sci-Fi Fan</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33780928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1265927100580624117">	Star Wars: republic Commando – Hard Contact is a great read for anybody who loves Star Wars books or Star Wars in general. The plot follows a squad of clone commandos and a Jedi Padawan as they carry out a mission on a farming planet. In this way, it is unlike most Star Wars books. It is a great military book, and shows how a four-person squad works together. The author uses imagery well to allow the reader to visualize the action and fighting. The book emphasizes working as a team a lot. It ke</span>
  <span id="freeText1265927100580624117" style="display:none">	Star Wars: republic Commando – Hard Contact is a great read for anybody who loves Star Wars books or Star Wars in general. The plot follows a squad of clone commandos and a Jedi Padawan as they carry out a mission on a farming planet. In this way, it is unlike most Star Wars books. It is a great military book, and shows how a four-person squad works together. The author uses imagery well to allow the reader to visualize the action and fighting. The book emphasizes working as a team a lot. It keeps repeating the importance, even in the dialogue. “Gdans work as a team. That’s what enables them to take prey that’s many times their size. A point, I think, that would not be lost on men such as yourselves.” However, it doesn’t just focus on military. Throughout the book, the characters relationships with each other grow as they get to know each other, and the author does a great job of portraying that through the character’s actions. The characters also connect to the reader with their various personalities and thoughts. I found this book to be a very interesting read, with a lot of action. It was very enjoyable, and I think and Science Fiction or Star Wars fan would like it as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1265927100580624117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Peter Martuneac" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33906751-peter-martuneac"><img alt="Peter Martuneac" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555974573p2/33906751.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3448964981?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter Martuneac" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter Martuneac" href="/user/show/33906751-peter-martuneac">Peter Martuneac</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3448964981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8673090167365566625">I’ve never read a Star Wars book before, so I don’t know if this one is pretty typical for how it goes but I really enjoyed it. It was far grittier than I expected, and the combat was described with surprising realism. The commandos faced hunger, fatigue, bad intel, all on top of the actual fighting. This wasn’t like the movie where a single Jedi can take down an entire regiment of droids.<br/><br/>Here we had 4 regular men and a young Padawan fighting tooth and nail (literally) to beat overwhelming odds </span>
  <span id="freeText8673090167365566625" style="display:none">I’ve never read a Star Wars book before, so I don’t know if this one is pretty typical for how it goes but I really enjoyed it. It was far grittier than I expected, and the combat was described with surprising realism. The commandos faced hunger, fatigue, bad intel, all on top of the actual fighting. This wasn’t like the movie where a single Jedi can take down an entire regiment of droids.<br /><br />Here we had 4 regular men and a young Padawan fighting tooth and nail (literally) to beat overwhelming odds and accomplish the mission. By the end of it, though mission accomplishment is still important, you can tell they’re no longer fighting for the Republic or any grand ideals. They fight for each other, and that’s as real as it gets.<br /><br />The author also did an incredible job adding military terminology and quips, and that’s rare. Their conversations and interactions never felt cheesy or forced. <br /><br />So this one gets 5 stars from me. I might have to get the next book in this particular series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8673090167365566625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3146635177" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3146635177" />
    <a title="Samantha (AK)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44733882-samantha-ak"><img alt="Samantha (AK)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438704616p2/44733882.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3146635177?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samantha (AK)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samantha (AK)" href="/user/show/44733882-samantha-ak">Samantha (AK)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11947565-hard-contact">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44733882-samantha-ak?shelf=setting-sf">setting-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44733882-samantha-ak?shelf=franchise-star-wars">franchise-star-wars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44733882-samantha-ak?shelf=genre-war">genre-war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44733882-samantha-ak?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3146635177" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1845125750004246916">Star Wars novels are my preferred brand of "junk food reading." I don't use that term because there's anything wrong with them; I used to consume them multiple times weekly. But it's franchise tie-in fiction. I know roughly what to expect, and I know there's a good chance I'll enjoy it, which makes it a good pick for cold, rainy weekends.<br/><br/><i>Hard Contact</i> is interesting, because its a deviation from the ol' Light vs Dark, Jedi vs. Sith conflict. Set shortly after the battle of Geonosis, it follows a </span>
  <span id="freeText1845125750004246916" style="display:none">Star Wars novels are my preferred brand of "junk food reading." I don't use that term because there's anything wrong with them; I used to consume them multiple times weekly. But it's franchise tie-in fiction. I know roughly what to expect, and I know there's a good chance I'll enjoy it, which makes it a good pick for cold, rainy weekends.<br /><br /><i>Hard Contact</i> is interesting, because its a deviation from the ol' Light vs Dark, Jedi vs. Sith conflict. Set shortly after the battle of Geonosis, it follows a squad of four Republic Commandos (special-ops Clone Troopers). It's a decent mil-SF book, but the real conflict here is moral. The clones think the Jedi are infallible; the Jedi don't know what to do about this brainwashed army they've been handled. You get to read this on both sides. There's also the backstory of the clones themselves, with some fairly well-done character voices.<br /><br />By far the greatest reason I can recommend this is that it takes the whole 'clone wars' situation seriously. It's easy not to think about it too hard in the movies, and the Clone Wars TV series (though entertaining) fails to hit the correct tone. This, though, is gritty without being gratuitous, and unflinchingly serious about what it takes to <i>make</i> a clone army, and what the results would be.<br /><br />A few minor issues:<br />1.) Jacket description says "chemical weapon," book contents say "nanovirus." Execution is iffy either way. Basically it's a doomsday macguffin.<br />2.) No matter how brutal the training the clones went through was, you cannot convince me that Niner 'didn't know how to scream.' These guys are still human; that's a major point of the book, even...<br />3.) The antagonist has got a couple of hangups over the idea of 'going soft,' especially with regard to women. He spends a lot of time reassuring himself that he's still a brutal shabuir of a Mandalorian even if he didn't kill somebody.<br />4.) There are a couple things that <i>really</i> haven't aged well, mainly an unfortunate shortening of "Trandoshan" and specism re: the alien Weequay characters<br /><br />Action-y junk food for a rainy weekend. Recommended to Star Wars fans interested in mixing things up a little.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1845125750004246916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6815870124057618731">I'll admit, all of the drama surrounding Karen Traviss has steered me away from these books. I've heard that she's ridiculously anti-Jedi and makes them out to be villains, and her track record of writing military fiction has made me think the RC books wouldn't be my favorites in the world. However, while this is only my first exposure, I think this book is truly phenomenal. The Clone Wars does a lot to show off how individualized clones really are, but not to this extent. Each character, clone </span>
  <span id="freeText6815870124057618731" style="display:none">I'll admit, all of the drama surrounding Karen Traviss has steered me away from these books. I've heard that she's ridiculously anti-Jedi and makes them out to be villains, and her track record of writing military fiction has made me think the RC books wouldn't be my favorites in the world. However, while this is only my first exposure, I think this book is truly phenomenal. The Clone Wars does a lot to show off how individualized clones really are, but not to this extent. Each character, clone and non-clone alike, feels very real and dynamic. I think her portrayal of the Jedi in this book is definitely thought-provoking and makes the reader think about their role in the war and how it might be opposed to Jedi ideology, but they're far from the villains. They're just more nuanced than they're generally made out to be I think. I'm very excited to continue with the series!<br /><br />Nitpicks: I don't like a lot of the names for things/people that Traviss chose all that much, and late in the book she felt that it was appropriate to use a transphobic slur as a nickname for Trandoshans. I really could've done without that.</span>

<div id="review_12302020" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12302020" />

I don't, however, feel that t</span>
  <span id="freeText13047196585427464178" style="display:none">I wanted to read this book because I fell in love with Karen Traviss' <i>wess'har</i> novels, so was definitely looking forward to reading pretty much anything else she writes. I'm also a Star Wars fan, but haven't read much in the Star Wars universe as far as tie-in novels but for one by Timothy Zahn a *long* time ago.<br /><br />It took me a long time to get into it - but once I did, I was drawn into Ms. Traviss' typical characterizations and didn't really want to put the book down. I don't, however, feel that this book was nearly as good as <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/124841.City_of_Pearl__Wess_har_Wars___1_" title="City of Pearl (Wess'har Wars, #1) by Karen Traviss" rel="nofollow noopener">City of Pearl</a>
</i> - the first book in her <i>wess'har</i> novels....so comparing "first" books - I feel this was not as rich in environment or in characterization.<br /><br />Would I read it again, though? *chuckle* Likely. Would I read on in the series? Definitely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13047196585427464178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1466609856232987744" style="display:none">I wish I could rate this higher, as it was well written, but the subject matter simply doesn't hold my interest. In terms of the Lucasverse, I'm definitely not ground-pounder/grunts/commandos/combat...I'm more capital ships/imperial shenanigans/Jedi/Old Republic history. That said, the two stars I am awarding are entirely because of the characters of Etain &amp; Darman. I want a novel featuring just the two of them...THAT would be my kind of read. I'm never going to get one...but I can dream.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3074441697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3074441697" />
    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1879990-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469320702p2/1879990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3074441697?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/1879990-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1879990-adam?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1879990-adam?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1879990-adam?shelf=legends">legends</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3074441697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer803362822382328558">Traviss' RC books were one of the few parts of Legends people generally recommended that I never bothered to try. They're definitely in the Orson Scott Card, Dan Simmons, "separate the art from the artist" category. And while Hard Contact certainly isn't anything on the level of Ender's Game, say, I don't think that comparison is totally unwarranted. It's a decent military scifi book, but as a Star Wars book, it has a lot of things I want that few other stories in the franchise deliver. <br/><br/>More tha</span>
  <span id="freeText803362822382328558" style="display:none">Traviss' RC books were one of the few parts of Legends people generally recommended that I never bothered to try. They're definitely in the Orson Scott Card, Dan Simmons, "separate the art from the artist" category. And while Hard Contact certainly isn't anything on the level of Ender's Game, say, I don't think that comparison is totally unwarranted. It's a decent military scifi book, but as a Star Wars book, it has a lot of things I want that few other stories in the franchise deliver. <br /><br />More than anything, it's a remarkably, even stubbornly, grounded and confined story. The farther I get from having played or read anything from the KOTOR era, the more I realize how cloying the scope and repetition of that Jedi v Sith story got to be. Honestly, there's a bit of it even in the ST. This is the furthest opposite of that. More even than Rogue One or The Mandalorian. Where Rogue One is a gritty war story laser-focused on the emotional stakes of its story, and The Mando is bluntly intertextual about its genre references, Hard Contact is relentlessly procedural, mostly about soldiers just walking around and hiding, noticing their environment, and adjusting tactics in response to new information and events. The story itself has nominal stakes, and they're clearly relevant to the theme of the story, but I'm not convinced they matter that much. Which is a good thing. The significance of this story comes almost entirely from the scenario itself, not its grander implications.<br /><br />Traviss' prose is certainly nothing special, and her characterization is not especially subtle. But after reading a few Disney canon novels that don't have character voices at all, it's enormously refreshing. Subtlety be damned, just give me a unique point of view. And for the most part, Hard Contact does that. The soldiers occasionally and the villain almost universally suffer from a touch of toxic masculinity, an internal monologue of boring brash tough-guy posturing. But even Hokan, the villain (who also suffers from a largely superfluous role in the story proper) was interesting and refreshing to me as a commander trying to do serious soldier work with droids. The Clone Wars goes so hard on making them jokes, making their commanders into cartoons, that the idea of simply being in those shoes, solving problems with those tools, is kind of revelatory on its own. <br /><br />The real interest, of course, lies in the Clones' perspectives and their relationship with the Jedi--both of which we see from both sides. The fact that accepting the aid of the Clone Army is a kind of moral abomination for the Jedi, equal to any of the individual atrocities that come downstream of the actual Clone War, arguably more acute even than the passive acceptance of slavery within and around the Republic. And yet, when I think about that, I tend to do it in abstract terms. They're technically slaves, technically children, engineered to lack autonomy, etc. What I had given less thought to was the direct atrocity of their creation. The best thing Traviss does, then, is to present the visceral trauma of the program that created the clones alongside the vulnerability and earnest trust they have toward the Jedi, and then mirror that back from Etain's perspective, learning for the first time what has been done on her behalf. <br /><br />It's a far more powerful and intimate microcosm of what Yoda goes through at the end of AotC: she's in a desperate situation, she has an important mission to achieve, and then someone just hands her these mysteriously competent men with the ability to solve all her problems. . . . with the caveat that accepting their aid means tacitly approving the things that were done to create them, and putting a bunch of trauma victims with minimal free will into harm's way. It's . . . quite rich, and the fact that Clone Wars barely scrapes the surface of this problem, or does it in such hamfisted ways, is really frustrating. The part that really makes it work, here and in general, is that the Jedi form these deep friendships with them. Despite the power dynamic, Etain sees herself in Darman, growing up in a creche, training to fight, given no choice in their role in society or even in the specific missions they are assigned. So they bond over that shared experience and use the parts of their experience that aren't shared to bolster each others' needs--Etain showing the compassion and humanity lacking in the clone training program, Darman teaching the instinctive self-confidence drilled into him by that inhumane training. <br /><br />The final choice Etain faces seems a bit abrupt and artificial, but I found it pretty interesting nonetheless. After the depth of her experience, Etain feels closer to the clones than to the Jedi, for better or worse, and that's a tension SW has rarely explored. Forcing her to choose between staying with the clones and letting them was a compelling take on the Jedi attachment doctrine, especially on top of the broader question of the disposability of clone lives. <br /><br />The worldbuilding elements, in the clone lingo, the Mandalorian culture, and the fauna and agronomy of Qiilura, are nothing special on their own, but they're all detailed and naturalistic enough to work. For a highly limited-POV Star Wars story, I could have used a bit more random nonsensical junk and weirdness. The thing that worked for me, again, were the side characters who developed that world. Even though Kal Skirata never shows up on screen, he's a well-drawn character just in the clones' recollections of him, which heavily color their own worldviews. He acts as another emotional perspective on them, a hardnosed soldier nominally less concerned with ethics than survival, but still kind of fucked up by the situation of these lads. <br /><br />Jinart is equally interesting. She acts as a foil to the clones and Etain, grounding the abstractions of their training and mission in the reality of a planet where people have lived and will keep living. She's weird and provocative and mysterious. It's a clear parallel to the "native guide" trope in colonial stories, since she is both an actual native and a highly competent tracker, but also secondary to the real heroes in the story. Wonder if there's any chance that will change in the later volumes. </span>
  <a data-text-id="803362822382328558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1750372084" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1750372084" />
    <a title="Keith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1061365-keith"><img alt="Keith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498062399p2/1061365.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1750372084?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Keith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Keith" href="/user/show/1061365-keith">Keith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1061365-keith?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1750372084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8560677146859351916">I never played the video game, so I don't know to what degree that changes my opinion. About half-way through this book, I thought this was a serviceable but very unremarkable addition to the Star Wars EU. The second half of the book changed my mind and I think this is an important piece of the EU. I'm reading through the EU again in chronological order and the Clone Wars novels are some that I've never read before. This was the first Star Wars book, show, game, or movie that confronted the mora</span>
  <span id="freeText8560677146859351916" style="display:none">I never played the video game, so I don't know to what degree that changes my opinion. About half-way through this book, I thought this was a serviceable but very unremarkable addition to the Star Wars EU. The second half of the book changed my mind and I think this is an important piece of the EU. I'm reading through the EU again in chronological order and the Clone Wars novels are some that I've never read before. This was the first Star Wars book, show, game, or movie that confronted the moral problems with creating clone soldiers. It's always been one of the huge problems with the movies in particular that the Jedi Order, the organization with the primary focus of doing what was right, didn't even bat an eye when an army of clone soldiers materializes. They never seem to even consider whether it is right to grow expendable human beings with shortened life span that you'll use as fodder in a war. <br /><br />This book doesn't avoid coming face to face with these questions, and it was refreshing. The lives of the clones is way darker in the book than previously seen. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[We hear about soldiers drowning in training while their teachers watch without caring. They train with live ammo being shot at them. We hear about entire batches of clones being executed because one sniper isn't exceptional. We also hear that you don't need to worry about clones getting old because, after a few years, they slow down a bit and they'll get taken care of in battle. They lived in fear of their Kaminoan makers because they would put them down in a heartbeat if they weren't perfect. Everything they eat is completely flavorless. This was a much more realistic and adult view that I think is more appropriate for the topic. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />I also really appreciated that Traviss had a more grown-up story in terms of decisions they would come across in a war. It wasn't like much of Star Wars story-telling from this time period where you have clear cut good guys who do good things and clear cut bad guys who casually do evil things. Those kinds of characters are just boring and childish. Especially in a war, good people are going to do questionable and sometimes horrific things. Traviss didn't do anything crazy here, but when she made <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Etain use a Jedi mind-trick to send the Weequay captain back to his former boss, where he was certainly going to be killed and most likely very slowly, in order to try and trick the enemy <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. That is the kind of decisions that most Clone Wars era storytellers I've come across have been to scared to handle. <br /><br />The story itself was decent, but the real take away from this book was the characters and their development. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[It would've been cool if she could've saved the Mandalorian villain Hokan for later use. He was a good villain. I liked that he wasn't a maniacal mustache twirler or some chaotic evil villain. He was a mercenary doing his job. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I got a kick reading a few other reviews, particularly the complaints. One review made me laugh really hard hearing the complaints about specie-ism and the use of a slur. You got to be a liberal if it ruins your day that an imaginary alien species isn't depicted in a favorable light in a book. I worry for her when she starts reading about Hutts. The slur was calling a Trandoshan a "trannie". Again, if that ruined your day, you must live in the greatest civilization in history. I noticed she didn't complain that they were a people that thrived on slavery. Oh well, I guess she was all out of outrage to worry about how the Trandoshan slave trade reflected on the historical slavery in the United States. <br /><br />I didn't care for the Gurlanins. They needed to be better described. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[They were shape shifters that by the end I figured out naturally run on four legs and have a snout.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> My biggest problem with them was <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[that they were insanely over powered. Jinart could shape shift into anything and just go wherever she wanted. It seems like if you have this superpower, you would make better use of it. I was very confused by her ability to turn herself into a black fluid and move around. Shape-shifting makes sense if you maintain body sizes, but how the heck do you turn yourself into and revert from a fluid? Wouldn't this allow you to go literally anywhere? <br /> Seems like she could've easily gone up the drain or infiltrated the installation very easily. <br /> Oh well. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8560677146859351916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3260716667" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3260716667" />
    <a title="Iset" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4973578-iset"><img alt="Iset" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563895104p2/4973578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3260716667?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iset" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iset" href="/user/show/4973578-iset">Iset</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781841495248" href="/book/show/349735.Star_Wars_Republic_Commando">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4973578-iset?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3260716667" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1880961621694474747"><br/>Going into this book, I wasn't filled with confidence. I’d already been burned by novelisations of The Clone Wars animated series, and I was beginning to see a pattern: that any Clone Wars era books based on what was originally other types of media tended to be not very good. Seeing Karen Traviss’ name on the cover was also slightly worrying given that I did not enjoy her novelisation of <i>The Clone Wars</i>. But hey, what the heck, I’m doing a massive re-read of all the Expanded Universe including th</span>
  <span id="freeText1880961621694474747" style="display:none"><br />Going into this book, I wasn't filled with confidence. I’d already been burned by novelisations of The Clone Wars animated series, and I was beginning to see a pattern: that any Clone Wars era books based on what was originally other types of media tended to be not very good. Seeing Karen Traviss’ name on the cover was also slightly worrying given that I did not enjoy her novelisation of <i>The Clone Wars</i>. But hey, what the heck, I’m doing a massive re-read of all the Expanded Universe including the few I missed, of which this was one. Let’s just give it a go.<br /><br />From that approach, I was pleasantly surprised. I saw another review that mentioned that you could easily skip ten chapters in the middle and not miss anything because the story veers off onto a diversionary path and only gets back to what is supposed to be the main plot later. And yes, you could do that. If you’re the kind of person who’s here for the military action and annoyed when the book goes off-track, then cutting out chapters 4 through to and including 11 might be for you. But it’s not for me, for the kind of reader that I am. See, I actually had the opposite experience.<br /><br />I’m not really a fan of military fiction. Don’t get me wrong, Traviss in this book writes it cleanly and clearly. But on the whole, no matter how well explained it is, I rarely engage with it because so very often the genre is functional and technical and never gives me a reason to care about why any of it is happening. So, I actually enjoyed those middle chapters of this book where we’re diverted off course from the military objective and thrown into unexpected situations where the protagonists have to explore and overcome unforeseen obstacles. It was much more interesting to me, and these were the chapters I enjoyed the most. Like I said, Traviss wraps things up cleanly once the plot does get back on course, but at that point, for me, it was almost a summary tying up of loose ends and finishing the book.<br /><br />Because the book managed to hold my interest decently through the majority of it, because it was cleanly (if not exceptionally) executed, I’m giving it a fairly decent rating. But why am I not rating it higher? That would be because, like most military fiction, it still failed to really make me care about its protagonists. The commando squad and Etain were just kind of blandly there, and I only really rooted for them because they’re the ‘good guys’ and not because I was struck by their fascinating personalities or anything. I know they’re brand new characters and it can be a struggle to make readers care about those in an established franchise, but other Expanded Universe books introduced original characters and succeeded in making me invest in them. This book didn’t. As for the secondary characters, they felt just roughly sketched. I didn’t dislike anyone, as such, but I just don’t really care for them either.<br /><br />Overall, better than I expected, but not exceptional enough to make it into my personal cut of canon.<br /><br /><b>6 out of 10</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="1880961621694474747" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11975274737987793595">By far one of the best Star Wars Universe series I've ever read. It may not be full of Jedi, but this story is Star Wars through and through. It puts into perspective everything that you didn't even consider, and more than makes up for the dreadful flops that were episodes I,II, and III. If you are at all interested in the back story of the clones, and understand that they're so much more than just bodies on the screen you should pick up these books. It blasts the "stupid stormtrooper" fallacy w</span>
  <span id="freeText11975274737987793595" style="display:none">By far one of the best Star Wars Universe series I've ever read. It may not be full of Jedi, but this story is Star Wars through and through. It puts into perspective everything that you didn't even consider, and more than makes up for the dreadful flops that were episodes I,II, and III. If you are at all interested in the back story of the clones, and understand that they're so much more than just bodies on the screen you should pick up these books. It blasts the "stupid stormtrooper" fallacy with its Deece, and vibroblades its way into the true canon of the Star Wars universe one book at a time.</span>

  <span id="freeText639234559735611445" style="display:none">OK, I'm going to make myself dead unpopular and come right out with it - I don't like Star Wars. Never have, probably never will. A brush with a Star Wars-based cult when I was a kid probably didn't help. <br /><br />However, I'd come across Karen Traviss's City of Pearl series, and thought her writing was the bee's knees, so I gave Hard Contact a whirl. Just, you know, in case. And, sure enough, while a lot of the background and Star Wars in-jokes made my eyes roll like cheap compasses, the writing was actually very good. She took a bunch of pieces that never impressed me much and made them into something greater than the sum of their parts.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2715075682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13179346797805586906">More like 3.5, I've heard of the author in plenty of space opera forums before and she didn't disappoint. The speech and brotherhood between the squad feels very palpable and real (along with the acronyms), and the progress of the story feels very calculated and well thought. The only problems I've had is how light the showdown was, a mandalorian versus clone commandos should have a LOT more juice to squeeze out. The look into the lives of the clones is awesome and builds a lot of depth to their</span>
  <span id="freeText13179346797805586906" style="display:none">More like 3.5, I've heard of the author in plenty of space opera forums before and she didn't disappoint. The speech and brotherhood between the squad feels very palpable and real (along with the acronyms), and the progress of the story feels very calculated and well thought. The only problems I've had is how light the showdown was, a mandalorian versus clone commandos should have a LOT more juice to squeeze out. The look into the lives of the clones is awesome and builds a lot of depth to their stories, something which only Clone Wars started delving not long ago, and adds a lot of value to the SW universe. Looking forward for the next ones!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13179346797805586906" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2244662294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3147732312797392815">Hard contact is about 4 identical clones of Jango Fett, and there padawan commander, Ethian, must destroy a virus that kills only clones and capture the head scientist. The problem is that the commando squad has been split up by a crash landing, and the mandolorian tasked with protect the scientist is hunting the padawan. Karen Traviss shows nerds every where the truth about Kaminoan society and culture, that the movies and TV show failed to show. Remember 99 from the Kaminoan episodes of the cl</span>
  <span id="freeText3147732312797392815" style="display:none">Hard contact is about 4 identical clones of Jango Fett, and there padawan commander, Ethian, must destroy a virus that kills only clones and capture the head scientist. The problem is that the commando squad has been split up by a crash landing, and the mandolorian tasked with protect the scientist is hunting the padawan. Karen Traviss shows nerds every where the truth about Kaminoan society and culture, that the movies and TV show failed to show. Remember 99 from the Kaminoan episodes of the clone wars? Well, not only would he not be a janitor, he would have been exterminated shortly after he was found "Genetically Imperfect". <br />A main theme of the book is "Not all soldiers were uniforms". Why do I think this you may be asking, well, because it's mentioned at least three times by the clones.<br />It was a fun and easy book to read. Would recommend it to any one who liked the CW show. <br /> </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1920632333" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1920632333" />
    <a title="Jared" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/738077-jared"><img alt="Jared" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530601058p2/738077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1920632333?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jared" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jared" href="/user/show/738077-jared">Jared</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/738077-jared?shelf=star-wars-legends-project">star-wars-legends-project</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/738077-jared?shelf=must-read">must-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1920632333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18022572059079548391"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/738077-jared?order=a&shelf=star-wars-legends-project&sort=position' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <b>Star Wars Legends Project #108</b>
</a><br/><br/><b>Background:</b> <i>Hard Contact</i> was written by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12672.Karen_Traviss' title='Karen Traviss' rel='nofollow noopener'>Karen Traviss</a> and published in October 2004. Traviss has written 10 Star Wars novels, in addition to a number of short stories and other materials. Most of her novels are set during the Clone Wars, but she also wrote 3 of the Legacy of the Force books. She has also written several Halo and Gears of War books, and half a dozen of her own science fiction novels.<br/><br/><i>Hard Contact</i> takes place about 3 months after the Battle of Geon</span>
  <span id="freeText18022572059079548391" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/738077-jared?order=a&amp;shelf=star-wars-legends-project&amp;sort=position" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>Star Wars Legends Project #108</b>
</a><br /><br /><b>Background:</b> <i>Hard Contact</i> was written by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12672.Karen_Traviss" title="Karen Traviss" rel="nofollow noopener">Karen Traviss</a> and published in October 2004. Traviss has written 10 Star Wars novels, in addition to a number of short stories and other materials. Most of her novels are set during the Clone Wars, but she also wrote 3 of the Legacy of the Force books. She has also written several Halo and Gears of War books, and half a dozen of her own science fiction novels.<br /><br /><i>Hard Contact</i> takes place about 3 months after the Battle of Geonosis, 22 years before the battle of Yavin. The main characters are a squad of clone commandos and a Jedi apprentice, none of whom have appeared previously elsewhere. Most of the story takes place on the Separatist-held planet Qiilura.<br /><br /><b>Summary:</b> Deployed on a mission to take out a Separatist research facility that is developing a virus targeting clones, four elite clone commandos (Darman, Niner, Atin, and Fi) find themselves in over even their very capable heads. They'll need all the help they can get, even from the likes of Etain Tur-Mukan, a young Jedi whose connection to the Force is tenuous at best, and Jinart, a mysterious Qiiluran native whose abilities are as mysterious as her motives.<br /><br /><b>Review:</b> So, this pretty much blew me away. And maybe part of that is because my expectations were really low for a novel that was written as a tie-in for a mediocre video game, but I honestly forgot all about that almost immediately. You'd never know that that's what this is. Traviss commits 100% to excellence and nothing about this feels like a phoned-in piece of merchandising. I love almost everything about this book, but let me try to focus in on four specific things that are great about it:<br /><br />First, Traviss writes with a tremendous amount of detail that feels "authentic" and natural, like she is totally familiar with this world and comfortable with every facet of it. The characters are fully realized and wonderfully drawn and a lot of fun to spend time with. But more importantly, her facility with dialogue and description makes the whole thing so immersive. This is top-notch military sci-fi, just set in the Star Wars universe.<br /><br />Second, Traviss has so many chances to quietly stack the deck in ways that would give us some cool action scenes and be perfectly fine and serviceable, but she never takes that easy way out. The primary antagonist is as fully-realized and compelling of a character as the protagonists, and he is every bit as smart as they are (which is so rare). He has his own advantages and disadvantages that come into play in the conflict that takes place over the course of the story. It's like Traviss is playing chess against herself, and she gives both sides the full benefit of her strategic expertise, so there's never a clear outcome telegraphed way in advance.<br /><br />Third, as I mentioned, none of these characters have ever appeared in any other stories, and most of them are, by definition, extremely expendable, so there's never a sense that anyone is guaranteed to survive to the end of the book. The tension is real because we care about these characters and it's pretty likely that at least some of them will end up dead. There aren't enough Star Wars novels that focus on "minor" characters like this, and when it's done well as it is here, it produces something really special.<br /><br />Fourth, Traviss takes some time to dig deep into the realities of warfare and the moral quandaries of the Republic using people, albeit cloned people, who are programmed not to care whether they live or die in waging that conflict. She also addresses the idea of collateral damage, and people who aren't as interested in the "higher ideals" of the Republic as they are in not losing everything and everyone that they care about in the crossfire of a galactic conflict whose outcome ultimately means very little to their daily lives.<br /><br />Just all around, in addition to being a well-written book with great characters and a great plot, this is one of those rare "Star Wars" stories that really lives up to the implications of that title. I loved it and I'm excited to read the rest of the series.<br /><br /><b>A</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="18022572059079548391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return

      Element.show('savingMessage')

    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;

      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''

    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer12730695287167228288">#1 New York Times best-selling novelist, scriptwriter and comics author Karen Traviss has received critical acclaim for her award-nominated Wess'har series, and her work on Halo, Gears of War, Batman, G.I.   <span id="freeText12730695287167228288" style="display:none">#1 New York Times best-selling novelist, scriptwriter and comics author Karen Traviss has received critical acclaim for her award-nominated Wess'har series, and her work on Halo, Gears of War, Batman, G.I. Joe, and other major franchises has earned her a broad range of fans. She's best known for military science fiction, but GOING GREY and BLACK RUN, the first books in her new techno-thriller series RINGER, are set in the real world of today. A former defence correspondent and TV and newspaper journalist, she lives in Wiltshire, England. She's currently working on SACRIFICIAL RED, the third book in the Ringer series, and sequels to THE BEST OF US in the NOMAD series, a collaboration with Galaxy's Edge. .</span>
